<!-- page 1 -->

Arabic Manuscripts Series
2

**Asas al-Ta'wil** (The Foundation of Interpretation)

Authored by the Ismaili Da'i al-Nu'man ibn Hayyun al-Tamimi al-Maghribi
Judge of the Fatimid Dynasty
Died 363 AH

Presented by Arif Tamir
Dar al-Tarqa Publications — Beirut

<!-- page 2 -->

<!-- page 3 -->

**Introduction**

I hesitated a long time before sending this book to print. The reason was that I wished to keep it longer in the cave of *taqiyya*, alongside the other Ismaili manuscripts that have not yet been published and circulated. That is one consideration. The other is that I believed my information about the book was not yet complete, and that other Ismaili manuscripts — lesser in rank than this one, but indispensable as scientific preliminaries to it, gateways through which one must pass before approaching it — still needed to appear in print before it. So I delayed it.

And now: whenever the word *ta'wil* (interpretation) comes to mind — the very subject we are about to discuss — only one book presents itself to me, *Asas al-Ta'wil*. It is the single book in the Ismaili manuscript corpus that takes up the subject of interpretation as its certain theme. It is the precious work that lays out the basic idea of this science in a measured and balanced way, presenting it with precision and detail, casting light on its sections and materials so as to bring them near to the mind and lower them within reach of thought. Interpretation is called *ta'wil* because it is a "return" (*awl*) to the outcome and to the origin of a thing — interpreting first what is and what is not, then bringing the matter back and resolving it, so that the fate of the words is their meaning and their substance.

In the earliest Ismaili da'wa, and in its flourishing,

<!-- page 4 -->

*ta'wil* was the primary theme of every esoteric philosophical idea — the tree that grew and branched into many branches, or more correctly the foundation upon which the pillars of this intellectual movement rested, the nourishment by which esoteric philosophy fed its judgment, its logic, and its exposition. For all of this, the book *Asas al-Ta'wil* is counted among the Ismailis as one of the precious books — a treasury whose doctrinal teachings demand the preservation of secrecy and the concealment of its lessons, and whose doctrinal materials must be guarded from leaking to anyone who is not Ismaili. All of it is regarded as the secret of the creed and the key to the door of the da'wa.

Added to this, the book contains an interpretation of the stories of the prophets that appear in the three heavenly books: the Torah, the Gospel, and the Qur'an. This too constitutes a subject whose full secrecy the doctrine demands be preserved, lest it pass beyond the circle of the general community, who are held to have grasped only the husks of culture and only the outward appearances of science.

It should be clear that *ta'wil* in its realistic Ismaili sense differs from *tafsir* (exegesis) in its conventional sense among the other Islamic sects. *Tafsir* means clarifying the meaning of any obscure word whose sense the reader does not understand. If we are asked, for example, "What is the *tafsir* of the word *tree*?" we answer: "It is a plant that is set down small, then grows, and from it limbs branch out upon which green leaves grow, and in spring it bears flowers that do not linger long before turning into good fruit," and so on. But if we are asked, "What is the *ta'wil* of the word *tree*?" the answer follows the view of the one giving the *ta'wil*: he may say it is a chamber, or a cow, or a rock, or anything else — provided it is in accord with truth, with reality, and with reason, that it is not foreign to belief, nor distant from sound

<!-- page 5 -->

thought. Thus *ta'wil* is the essence of the meaning, its symbol, or its inner reality — a truth hidden behind a word that does not openly signify it. Hence the Ismaili intellectual order assigns the authority of *tanzil* (revelation) to "the Speaker" (*al-Natiq*), and the authority of *ta'wil* to the Imam. The first is taken to represent the Sharia — rulings, jurisprudence, law — the apparent (*zahir*); the second represents the truth, the interpretation, the philosophy, the inward (*batin*).

It is recognized that *ta'wil* is one of the sciences the Ismailis singled out for their community, and which they named "esoteric" for that very reason. They held that Muhammad was the bearer of the revelation of the Qur'an — as we have said — and that Ali was the bearer of its interpretation: that is, the Qur'an was revealed to Muhammad in its words and its outward sense for the people at large, while its mystical secrets were entrusted to Ali.

The Ismailis took certain verses of the Noble Qur'an as proof of the obligatory standing of interpretation. He said:

"Thus your Lord shall choose you and teach you the interpretation of events."

And He said:

"And thus We established Joseph in the land, that We might teach him the interpretation of events."

And He said:

"I shall inform you of the interpretation of that which you could not bear patiently."

And He said:

"He it is who has sent down to you the Book, in which are verses precisely defined — they are the foundation of the Book — and others ambiguous. As for those in whose hearts is deviation, they pursue what is ambiguous of it, seeking discord and seeking its interpretation, though none knows its interpretation save

<!-- page 6 -->

Allah and those firmly grounded in knowledge. They say: 'We believe in it; the whole of it is from our Lord.' But none take heed except those of understanding."

There are rational proofs for the necessity of interpretation drawn from the Holy Qur'an as well. The Almighty said:

"We shall show them Our signs on the horizons and within themselves."

And He said:

"And in the earth are signs for those certain in faith — and in your own selves: do you not see?"

All of this makes clear that the apparent was created in order to point to the subtle. The Ismailis took the apparent as a parable. Their great supporter of religion, the foremost of their preachers and philosophers — al-Mu'ayyad fi al-Din — says on this subject:

"Allah has made parables and likenesses: the human body is a likeness, and the soul is a likeness; this world is a likeness, and the Hereafter is a likeness."

He also said:

"By the parable I mean a parable struck — like the bee's needles, and what comes from it like honey."

All of this carries us to the city of Alexandria and to its philosophical school — to what Philo and his disciples said when they tried to interpret the Torah esoterically but did not succeed. Augustine and his disciples then tried to follow his school in interpreting the Gospel esoterically, but they too could not bring the idea to completion, for reasons too long to set out here. Then came the Ismailis, who spread their system of thought; and this was the first curriculum they put forward and to which they called others — a system of interpretation. Plainly, they refined it with their own ideas and added to it a rational interpretation that pruned bare transmission and produced conclusive proof

<!-- page 7 -->

for the Islamic world. They are the most ancient in their understanding of Islamic principles. They affirmed the inward and its necessity, just as they affirmed the outward alongside it: the apparent is not accepted without the inward, and the inward is of no use without the apparent — for outward and inward are like body and soul, and benefits arise only from their meeting, through which the ends are known.

In any case, it must also be said that *tanzil* — as we have noted — was specialized in by the Prophet during his lifetime: it is the knowledge of the apparent and of the rulings of the Sharia, the knowledge to which the public is permitted access, which they may learn, adopt, and embrace as their station. As for the *imam*, the foundational figure (*al-asas*) — a designation given to every Imam contemporary with "the Speaker" — he is responsible for the inward, for the interpretation, for the truth and its secret symbols and teachings, which may not be disclosed except to the loyal soldiers of the da'wa who have risen in its ranks and been elevated in its stations. These are the "specially favored" classes.

For these reasons we see that the two books *Asas al-Ta'wil* and *Da'a'im al-Islam* — both authored by al-Nu'man ibn Hayyun — represent two related and fully interconnected ideas and directions. By them I mean the inward and the outward: the first is regarded as the foundation of esoteric philosophy, the second as the foundation of jurisprudence and the Sharia, narrated by Imam Ja'far ibn Muhammad al-Sadiq from his grandfather, the Prophet Muhammad — for the Prophet is the bearer of the Sharia, of the rulings of the outward and the revealed, as we have said. This furnishes a conclusive proof of the book's importance, and it shows that Ismaili esoteric studies proceeded by degrees, and that their ranks were divided into ranks through which the respondent must pass and whose circles he must complete.

It must further be said that the Ismailis were alone in interpreting the verses of the Qur'an that require interpretation, where other sects content themselves with mere *tafsir*. All of this is plain in the books of the earliest Qur'anic commentators who preceded Muhammad ibn Jarir al-Tabari — whose *tafsir* is the great

<!-- page 8 -->

scientific tribunal for weighing among Islamic sects and for testing the soundness of theories and ideas.

We return to the heart of the matter, repeating that it is very difficult to understand and to reach the end of Ismaili philosophical texts, to grasp their expressions, their symbols, and their aims, unless the reader has first passed through the foundational interpretive texts. There is no doubt that *Asas al-Ta'wil* is one of the texts that must be studied carefully and whose content must be understood.

It should not be hidden from researchers or from interested readers that al-Nu'man ibn Hayyun wrote many books in jurisprudence, law, rulings, history, and in the science of interpretation — which is considered an introduction (1) to philosophy. We have not, however, observed that he wrote any books on philosophy itself, and we do not know whether he was forbidden to do so. What is known to us is that Ja'far ibn Mansur al-Yaman was directly responsible for that field in that period, and was the only one with permission to present it, to write about it, and to discuss its topics. Yet none of this prevents us from saying that al-Nu'man is counted among the great thinking minds who laid the pillars of thought in the Fatimid state.

Yes — and there is no exaggeration in declaring it — the Ismailis had their share, indeed the most advanced share, in this field. They won the first prize in the arena of thought, and they enriched the Islamic library with a massive scientific production before the existence of the Fatimid state. Unfortunately, that precious heritage has been lost, and nothing remains in our hands today except the *Rasa'il Ikhwan al-Safa* (the Epistles of the Brethren of Purity) alone.

(1) The names of al-Nu'man's works are mentioned in *Uyun al-Akhbar* by its author Idris Imad al-Din (6/36–39); it is reported that he wrote forty-two books, to which Shaykh Isma'il added two more in his index. Ibn Khallikan translated his life in *al-Wafayat* (2/166); and al-Hafiz in *Raf' al-Isr* (136); and the Da'i Idris Imad al-Din in *Uyun al-Akhbar*, part six. See also the supplement to al-Kindi's *Book of Governors and Judges* (586), Gottheil's article in JAOS (27/217), Husayn Hamdani's article in JRAS (1933/368–370), Muhammad Kamil Husayn's introduction to *Kitab al-Himma* (5–14), Asaf Fyzee's article in JRAS (1934/1–32), Ivanow (37–40), and Arif Tamir's introduction to *Khams Rasa'il Ismailiyya* (Five Ismaili Letters), 1956.

<!-- page 9 -->

There is another point worth noting, and one which occupies a long stretch of our thought: the subject of the Ismaili literature that existed before the Fatimid state. There is no doubt that systems of interpretation and philosophical-ideological laws prevailed in their society in that period; and we see that we have the right to wonder where those intellectual fruits went, and what the names are of those who produced them.

The answer to this question can be very difficult. In reality, we do not possess, from those earlier eras laden with events, any trace of the Ismailis except the *Rasa'il Ikhwan al-Safa*, as we have said. It is likely that those antiquities were destroyed at the da'wa center at Salamiyya, in Syria, during the raids of the Abbasids, the Qaramita, and other groups. For this reason, the information about the poet Abd al-Salam ibn Raghban — known as *Dik al-Jinn* (the Rooster of the Jinn) — and about al-Mutanabbi, al-Ma'arri, and others, has remained scant and has not lit the way before researchers nor informed the interested reader.

In any case, we must refer to the story related by Ja'far al-Hajib in his biography, published by the orientalist Ivanow, concerning Muhammad al-Mahdi — Ubaydallah — the founder of the Fatimid state in the Maghrib (North Africa): how some thieves attacked him on his way from Salamiyya to the Maghrib and stripped him of the collection of precious books he had carried with him from the Levant, and how the Fatimid Imam *al-Qa'im* then returned to that very place, once the Fatimids had settled their affairs in the Maghrib, and recovered the books. This indicates that

<!-- page 10 -->

the collection — which he had himself selected, carried, valued, and preferred above gold and precious stones — was of great worth; and there is no doubt that it contained a system of esoteric beliefs, the rules of philosophy, of interpretation, and of other philosophical topics.

We used to say yesterday, and we return and repeat today, that the third and fourth centuries of the Hijra are among the most splendid of ages for the Arab and Islamic worlds. During these centuries the Ismaili da'wa flourished in a comprehensive intellectual flowering. Into the arena of knowledge the Ismaili preachers stepped, free of every external influence, restriction, or consequence, and worked to transmit much of the sciences of the Persians, of India, and of Greece — after expounding them, adapting them, and refining them — placing the scattered theories in order and applying them in a manner suited to Islamic principles and to reason, with transmission as their tool. All of this took place during the early Ismaili da'wa, when they were saturated with the science, the disputation, and the debates that were spreading very swiftly throughout the world. To this must be added their firm grip on the apparent of the Sharia — its obligations, its duties, its rulings — a realistic adherence which they would not tolerate being abandoned or neglected, joined to the urgent religious desire to hold also to the esoteric scientific worship alongside it.

All of this is in addition to the dangerous role the Ismailis played in political, social, and intellectual life. In truth, there is no group within Islam that has so long a record in events as the Ismailis.

There is no doubt that the Buyids and the Hamdanids raised the banner of culture and literature high in the Levant, and that the Umayyads, too, made an effective contribution to building the literary renaissance in Andalusia. But all of that does not equal a small part of what the Fatimids contributed in this field, especially during the era of Imam *al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah*, who


<!-- page 11 -->
History counts his era as one of the most prosperous periods Islam ever knew. Al-Mu'izz al-Fatimi possessed great understanding and farsightedness, and he was deeply committed to reviving the scientific renaissance throughout his vast realm and to strengthening the intellectual system he considered the foundation of his da'wa. It is not surprising, then, that we find him personally contributing — collaborating with the da'i al-Nu'man on two books that together form the basis of every science, of jurisprudence, and of philosophy. The first was meant as the foundation of the apparent law — what they call "practical worship" — namely *Da'a'im al-Islam* (The Pillars of Islam). The second was meant as the foundation of esoteric philosophy, or "scientific worship" — namely the basis of ta'wil (interpretation). As the first laid down the law, the rulings, and the body, the second laid down the truth, the philosophy, and the spirit.

Yes — Imam al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah contributed to the revival of the Fatimid state in the intellectual sphere. He raised it scientifically and literarily to a height no Fatimid Imam before or after him had reached. In his time culture reached its peak, the literary market flourished, and Cairo became a Ka'ba toward which scholars, writers, philosophers, students, and seekers turned — drawing from its flowing springs the various sciences and arts. In this field the Fatimid state surpassed its competitors in learning and literature just as it had surpassed them politically and militarily.

When we recall that al-Mu'izz himself was a highly cultivated intellectual who had absorbed a vast measure of knowledge, we understand why. Beyond his command of the dominant languages of his age — Latin, Italian, and Spanish, which it is said he learned because he intended to conquer those countries and annex them — he also studied Sicilian, Berber, and Sudanese, given the considerable numbers of slaves and soldiers from those regions in his armies. On top of all this, he was a man devoted to poetry and literature, as the historians who recorded his life relate.

<!-- page 12 -->
Al-Maqrizi and other historians say his library overflowed with books. He delighted in convening scholarly councils, in listening to the debates of the learned, in encouraging them and providing them with funds, and he personally supervised authors and their works, ordering changes and refinements.

It is worth noting that al-Nu'man ibn Muhammad ibn Mansur ibn Ahmad ibn Hayyun — the author of our book, *Asas al-Ta'wil* (The Basis of Interpretation) — was born in Morocco, into a family belonging to the Arab tribe of Banu Tamim. We have found no mention of the date of his birth in his biography, but it is known that he died in Cairo in the year 363 AH, during the imamate of al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah al-Fatimi. Al-Nu'man is truly the father of a family that served learning with distinguished service to the Ismaili da'wa, performing every possible scholarly service for it; the prolific output of this family shaped the course of public thought and mental life not only in Egypt but throughout the lands of Islam.

It is fair to say that he is the most famous jurist Ismailism has produced since the dawn of Islam, and among its most productive — the most prolific in substance, the most fertile, the most generative of books on jurisprudence and on legal and religious research. He entered the service of Imam Muhammad al-Mahdi (Ubayd Allah), founder of the Fatimid state in Morocco, in the late years of that Imam's life. Then, under the Fatimid (al-Qa'im), he took charge of the judiciary in Tripoli in the west. In the era of Imam (al-Mansur) he was appointed judge of (al-Mansuriyya), where his scholarly assemblies, his mosques, and his literary councils and lectures captivated the hearts of those who sought knowledge and elevation. When the Imam al-Mu'izz li-Din Allah came to Egypt he brought al-Nu'man and his family with him, intending the state to benefit from him in Egypt as it had benefited from him in Morocco. But while we speak of al-Nu'man, we must ask:

<!-- page 13 -->
what of his literary production in Morocco? Did the same fate that befell so many works at the hands of the angles of the ages befall his too?

Finally, we must turn to the central theme — ta'wil (interpretation) — and say:

This science was propagated by the Ismailis among the various classes of Muslims with great care. It is, in truth, the natural outcome of their foundational learning and their intellectual system. In their view, religion required that a person ascend through continuous discipline, rising from one degree to the next, until at last he attained knowledge of the houses of the universe — the inhabited worlds that were cut off after they were separated from the transcendent Creator: that is, from Absolute Non-Anthropomorphic Thought, the First Intellect, the Higher Light, or the Originating Power — the Pen that writes, the First that precedes existence, the one who emits lights from itself toward the Second Place, toward the Second Universal Intellect, or toward the Universal Soul. These two principles are the two origins from which everything proceeds, after their transformation in the minds of human beings — the minds of the prophets, the imams, and the elect. The common classes, however, do not possess such distinct minds; they are destitute of them so long as they have not acquired knowledge or moved to the second station. That movement comes through enlightenment and guidance, and this kind of education has many degrees. If a person walks them, he may reach the ultimate degree of mental and literary perfection that is the highest purpose of human life. To achieve this end, one must work on the development of the mental faculties and then on the cultivation of a good biography and a literary life that accords with the demands of sound reason and the theory of intellectual development that leads a man to belief — the perfect believer, the wise one, the one stripped clean.

This system of ta'wil grew in just this way, leading to just such a theory, because the Ismailis gave the religious laws and the Qur'anic verses an esoteric interpretation that differed from their outward sense and yet conformed to common sense. At the same time, they did not reject the revealed laws and did not deny what the Qur'an and the other heavenly books brought.

<!-- page 14 -->
Rather, they affirmed them and acknowledged that their obligations could not be set aside; and they understood what God meant by them, for they were among those whose insight had been opened by knowledge. They thus separated into the ranks of the da'wa of the religious world, which is the type of the world of creators and created beings.

From this we may conclude that the Ismailis do not operate by apparent interpretation alone. They interpret the laws, the rulings, and the Qur'an esoterically as well, drawing from a trusted source they consider to be the representative of the Universal Intellect. We may therefore call them rationalists who anchor their beliefs only on these foundations. Beyond that, the Ismaili movement is a universal one — or, more plainly, an "intellectual system" — whose mission was to overthrow the dominant political order that controlled the Islamic world and to achieve a major upheaval in ideas, systems, and beliefs.

Yes — this system of thought required of its adherent that he affirm both the apparent and the subtle principles, for the two principles together constitute the spirit of religion. They are joined to each other in full conjunction, and both are obligatory upon every respondent and adherent. The apparent is the general framework of recommendations bound up with the rules of religious scholarship; the subtle is the spirit of religion hidden from view, reserved for the dedicated. For this reason the Ismaili preachers proclaimed their principles in two modes and summoned others to their intellectual system through two ideas.

The first concerned the prophecies and the necessity of accepting what they brought — laws, texts, and ordinances — in an apparent way, calling people to follow them in the manner of "obligation and imitation."

The second was a call to what the heavenly books had brought through

<!-- page 15 -->
esoteric interpretation, in the manner of "choice, covenant, and pledge."

From this it may be said that the Ismaili system of thought was founded on lofty fundamental philosophical teachings, which stipulate following the idea of pure, absolute faith, thirsting after knowledge, and never relying on what others repeat — thoughts that echo onward without understanding.

Religion was and still is in a fortified citadel that cannot be breached; one cannot reach what lies behind it except through a real, abiding spiritual meaning — an essence subsisting in itself — and never through gleaming external appearances and superficial teachings that, with the passage of time, become mere habit. For whatever the mind does not register, or what lies outside the mind's compass, is in origin nonexistent and does not endure.

The verses of the Qur'an were revealed openly, and yet they were not devoid of esoteric meaning, of soul, of essence — present in every being. It is worth noting that the texts of the Qur'an, or its tafsir, often lead to differences of opinion, while ta'wil — the disclosure of the esoteric meaning — is a fixed system that does not shift; it leads to mental reality and to the consensus of society.

We have said that ta'wil — which is "knowledge" — was the specialty of the Imam and his disciples. For this reason we see the Prophet (Muhammad) keen to commend to his companions the search for it wherever it may be found.

The proof of this is that the knowledge of the apparent is very easy to reach; ta'wil, by contrast, cannot be reached except after struggle, weariness, and the surmounting of obstacles — after passing through trials and entering the ranks of an intellectual system from which one does not emerge

<!-- page 16 -->
without being marked by what they have called "the covenant, the compact, and the oath."

In summary, it must be said that God revealed His religion to humanity and gave it the optional portion by way of following the clearest paths, demonstrating its justice and leaving freedom before man to act in the light of the intellect of his constitution — making that intellect the finest gift bestowed upon him.

Islam, in fact, was nothing other than the forbidding of evil and the permitting of the good. Its inner reality is absolute faith — the inward spirit that runs in the blood of every human being and aims at abstraction and at living in the shadow of idealism and virtue.

Whatever the case, we can also say, beyond all that we have said, that ta'wil is a philosophical and religious theory which may be summarized thus: God Almighty has set all the meanings of religion in the creatures that surround man; one must therefore substitute what is in nature and on the face of the earth, according to one's understanding of the truth of religion, which has divided creation into two categories: an apparent part that is visible and an inward part that is hidden, with the apparent pointing to the inward — like the human body, which is apparent, and the soul, which is inward. What is disclosed from the matters of religion is practical worship, and what is set down in the apparent verses of the Qur'an consists of meanings known and articulated by the scholars of the people of the apparent and by the common public alike. Yet each of the religious duties also has an esoteric interpretation, which can only be taught by the imams, by their senior preachers, and by the elect.

In any case, the Holy Qur'an stands before us, and it makes clear that the creed Islam has brought is not contrary to reason, nor should it conflict

<!-- page 17 -->
with reason — nor, indeed, is it complete if it does not square with reason — particularly since the Islamic religion was born a rational religion capable of development.

If we survey the Qur'anic verses and analyze what is mentioned in them in the light of reason, logic, and reality, they teach us that man must think, reflect, look, meditate, consider, and deduce. Islam is a comprehensive worldly order that directs man in life and helps him attain — for himself and for the human collective — the highest degree of human perfection in spirit, character, matter, and essence.

Knowledge in Islam has been made a condition of faith: whoever believes without understanding and knowing has not believed well. True belief is the truth that emerges from guidance, from understanding, and from choice. We may say, then, that Islam is a revolution against mental rigidity and a war upon reaction, fanaticism, and constriction. It granted freedom to step out, to loosen and unfetter; it released freedom of opinion and thought, raised it to a high station, and fenced it about with care and attention. The Islamic creed rests upon argument and evidence, not upon compulsion. It honored the intellect, wanted it alert, and demanded that it be attentive. It was not acceptable that a person be a deaf machine accepting whatever he was told without ruling on it by his own mind.

Islam also freed human minds from the constraints of imitation, opening before them a space to walk in the direction of guidance, in a wide atmosphere of freedom — settling a sound mind in them and setting it apart from its opponents, especially in matters of research, instruction, and discussion.

<!-- page 18 -->
Thus did Islam mean to awaken the mind and rouse it to perform its mission, to develop its mental and intellectual powers, that it might walk in the field of self-esteem and independence of opinion and move toward freedom of thought.

This is a brief presentation of the subject of (ta'wil) as set forth in the book (Asas al-Ta'wil) by al-Nu'man ibn Hayyun al-Tamimi. We have dealt with it in all its aspects, drawing upon our Ismaili materials enough to suffice, while keeping in mind that the legal code of the Ismaili da'wa is the only source that guides researchers to distinguish the intruders from the genuine envelopers of the tradition. Before reaching the end, I must offer a word to those who call themselves specialists in the study of Ismaili literature, history, and philosophy — those who have not yet reached the point of distinguishing between the two terms (Ismailiyya) and (Fatimiyya). To these we say: we are not convinced, because we have found no mention of the name "Fatimiyya" in our studies. We are sure that the name should be confined to the family that ruled the Ismailis — namely (the Imams) — and has nothing to do with the philosophy itself. From this it follows that the use of the word "Fatimid" should not extend beyond the Fatimid dynasty during its reign in Morocco and Egypt. The reason is well known: their wish to draw near to the Islamic world, which honors the name of Fatima al-Zahra, daughter of Muhammad, and, on the other hand, to distinguish themselves from the descendants of 'Ali by women other than Fatima. So every name applied to literature, philosophy, doctrine, or anything else pertaining to this da'wa must not stray beyond the scope of the word (Ismailiyya).


Contents of the Book

Al-Nu'man divided his book (*Asas al-Ta'wil* — The Basis of Interpretation) into six chapters, devoting one chapter to each Speaker-Prophet (*natiq*), the chief of his cycle, his preachers, and his followers. Each chapter is filled with Quranic verses concerning their stories, their era, and the events of their time. He begins with the era of Adam and ends with the era of Muhammad.

He did not write the chapter concerning the Awaited Imam — the seventh — because his time has not yet come.

In my judgment, it would be very difficult to explain the chapters of the book one by one. Nevertheless, there is one chapter that deserves particular attention: the chapter dealing with 'Isa ibn Maryam (Jesus, son of Mary) and the story of his birth without a father, followed by the story of Adam and the meaning of his creation from clay. All of this is worth reading and reflecting on, for it is innovative in its philosophy of doctrine, rich in ideas, and marked by sound rational maturity.

Copies of the Book

I obtained two manuscripts of *Asas al-Ta'wil*. The first came from the town of Masyaf in Syria, and it was the principal manuscript adopted in the present edition; it is designated by the letter (S), and was copied in 1183 AH. The second came from manuscripts of Yemen, presented to me by my honored brother Hakim Luqman of the town of Kampala in Uganda, East Africa, during my visit to that country in 1956. It is written in the hand of Amin ibn 'Ali al-Kamenhab Dari Watana al-Shakadhakar, and was copied in 1033 AH; it is designated by the letter (e).

It is worth mentioning that we have made grammatical and orthographic corrections, restored some sentences that had dropped out, and revised Quranic verses that had been corrupted in transmission. We have not noted these emendations in the margins, and they do not depart from the substance of the original. All of this was done in order to produce a sound text upon which no dust settles.

In this work we have rendered a service to scholarship and discharged our duty toward our history, our literature, and Arabic philosophy.

Aref Tamer
Beirut, Lebanon
1960

Author's Introduction

In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.

Praise be to Allah, Bestower [^1] of grace, and may Allah bless Muhammad, the Prophet of mercy, the *wasi* (executor) appointed for the *umma*, and the pure Imams from his goodly progeny.

To proceed: with the support of Allah, His gratitude, His help, His bounty, and His benevolence, we have already published a book for those who respond to the call of truth, who cling to the rope of the Creator of creation, who hold fast to the trustworthy handhold, and who adhere to the straightest path — a book treating the outward knowledge of the *shari'a*. We called it *Da'a'im al-Islam* (The Pillars of Islam) [^2]. In it we set forth the limits of faith and the distinction between *iman* and *islam*, the obligation of *walaya*, the proofs of the Imams, the requirements of duty, the chapters of the lawful and the forbidden, and the questions and rulings — so that those who respond to the call might know the matter that is outward in their religion, believe in it, act by it, and neither contradict it nor abandon it. For this is the first frontier of education and the lowest degree of knowledge and understanding.

The one in whom the spirit of faith and wisdom is to be inscribed must begin from this point, and must pass from the realm of darkness into the realm of light. This is the first stage for the one newly born in faith — the stage of nurture at birth, when the spirit of life is breathed into him. Just as the newborn emerges from the womb into the light of the world, and his outward form is then repaired by the cutting of the umbilical cord [^3] — which had been the link between what was and what was not yet of him — and his limbs are adjusted, his skin anointed, and he is swaddled with what strengthens and tightens him, lest his body slacken and his limbs become deformed, his joints weakened, his constitution distorted, or some other ruin overtake him —

So too is the case of the one who does not repair the outward of his religion and establish it: he is on the verge of breaking his religion and ruining it. We therefore furnished those who responded to the call with what they need in order to repair and rectify themselves. We read it out to them; we put it in the hands of those who wished to copy it; and we did not deny it to those who wished to recite it in full. Each took from it according to his measure, and some — though they were few — benefited from it, in accordance with the words of the Almighty:

> "Except for those few who believed and did righteous deeds — and how few they are."

And:

> "But most of them are insolently disobedient and do not hear."

And of most of them, He said:

> "But most people do not know."

The Almighty also said:

> "How many people, even if you were eager, will not believe."

And:

> "Among them are the believers, but most of them are transgressors."

The Almighty said:

> "Among them are those who lie in wait, ignorant of the light."

They said: this is the outward, which we know — and what we see of it is useful to us. They turned away from it in disgust, saying that this is the speech of those whom Allah has reported as having lied to His friends, the Messengers, in the verse:

> "Glorified is the Almighty and exalted."

And those who disbelieved said: this is the man whom others have helped unjustly and falsely. And they said:

> "These are nothing but fables of the ancients which he has written down; they are dictated to him morning and evening."

This is one of the ways of denying the limits of religion. They corrupted what was meant for the ancients and others — what the evil preachers thought of those who turned them away from the friends of Allah — and they departed from what their friends had thought concerning them, disobeyed their command, altered the order that had been arranged for them, transgressed their limits, and prevented others from fulfilling their duty.

> "They went astray before, and they led many astray, and they strayed from the right way."

Had they obeyed the command of their guardians and followed the path of the nation as set out for them, they themselves would have been guided, and no others would have had any objection. And Allah will set right the foolish one He sees fit, restoring whatever of the religion of God he changed, and repairing what those who disobeyed Him have corrupted — by the power of Allah, His might, His provision, and His mercy, and through the success He grants to whoever preserves what We have set forth for him.

The measure of the outward, and the share that each receives of it — though they be few of many — has not moved us to neglect them. We have resolved to raise them to the next stage of the limits of the responding initiate (*mustajib*): the stage of symbol (*ramz*) and sign (*ishara*), which is the gentlest and most accessible of its degrees.

This is parallel to the raising of children and the nourishment of their bodies. After repairing their outward form at birth, they are begun on milk — because of the delicacy of their constitution before the possibility of solid food, and their inability to digest it until their bodies grow strong enough to bear it and be ready for it, taking it gradually. So too with symbols and signs: they impregnate the imaginations of the listeners [^4], who are stirred by what is symbolized to them and pointed to by wisdom, and who are eager to hear it and ready to accept it — whereas if the same content were thrust upon them all at once, without preamble, and arrived upon them suddenly when their forces of comprehension [^5] were not yet equal to it, it would harm them, just as feeding solid food to children prematurely harms them. Allah the Almighty has defined this when He said:

> "And mothers shall suckle their children two full years, for those who wish to complete the suckling."

Therefore, after the book *Da'a'im al-Islam*, we composed for those whom we have mentioned a book that we called *Hudud al-Ma'arifa* (The Limits of Knowledge) [^6]. In it we established the limits explaining the proof of *ta'wil* (interpretation) against the one who denied it, and of the inner sense (*batin*) against the one who rejected it, and the manner of ascending its degrees. In it we set forth those symbols and signs of the *batin* and *ta'wil* which benefit the people of intellect — those whose way is the way of acceptance, desire, withdrawal from worldly attachments, and asceticism.

When we had reached a certain measure in that work, we were obliged by those who desired to attain what they deserved to compose a further book unfolding for them a degree of *ta'wil* and subtlety beyond the degree of symbol. Whoever hears anything of it that contradicts its proper limits as we have set them forth, and does not act accordingly — referring it through himself to other than what he has heard, or interpreting it without the prior explanation that has been given — his way must be the way of those we have just mentioned: those who submit themselves to those who came before them, follow in their path, stand in their footsteps, and let themselves be clothed in the religion of their judgment.

> "That Allah may test those who believe and destroy the disbelievers."

— as the Almighty said, and He is the most truthful of those who speak. If they had only understood the speech of the truthful one [^7], the trustee — for it reached them when he initiated some of his preachers (*du'at*) into it. It was that a man said to him: "O son of the Messenger of Allah, we have heard from you before this time something other than this — a different aspect?" He — namely Ja'far al-Sadiq — said:

> "I speak one word with seven faces."

The man, astonished, said: "Seven, O son of the Messenger of Allah?" He said: "Yes — and seventy. And were we to add more, we would add."

This knowledge has been displayed in proportion to its limits, so that the one who has heard it and benefited from it may know it page by page [^8] in its degrees. This present book, which we have set forth in its foundational form — being the first of its limits — we have called *Asas al-Ta'wil* (The Basis of Interpretation). By it we mean to explain what we established in *Da'a'im al-Islam*, so that this work may serve as the foundation for the *batin*, just as that work was the foundation for the *zahir* (the outward). With Allah we seek help — there is no power and no strength except in Allah the Almighty.

Chapters of Discourse on the Interpretation of Interpretation,
and on the Inner Meaning of What Is Mentioned in Hadith and Revelation

In *Hudud al-Ma'arifa*, the book we have already named, we expanded at length in establishing the science of *ta'wil* and *batin*, and in answering those who denied them from the Book, the Sunna, and the sayings of the Imams and the Community. We have said enough there, even briefly. Yet that does not absolve us from publishing this present book on the side of the *batin*, so that it may stand as our proof in the hands of those who recite it. So we say — and from Allah comes success:

For every intelligible thing there must be an outward and an inward. Its outward is what the senses perceive; its inward is what it contains — the knowledge that resides within it, and the meaning that its outward form embraces. The two are each other's spouse and partner [^9]. Allah the Almighty said:

> "And He has lavished upon you His blessings, outward and inward."

He thereby informed His servants that He will question them concerning His blessings upon them, and said:

> "Then on that Day you will be asked about the bliss."

Whoever does not know what the inner bliss is — though he knows that he is held responsible for it and how he should answer if he is questioned about it — how will he then answer? And the Almighty said:

> "And forsake the outward and inward of sin."

Whoever does not know the secret of sin, and is therefore warned of it, is he not in danger of falling into it precisely because he does not know it? Then He mentioned in His book, saying:

> "It is He who has sent down to you the Book; in it are verses precise in meaning — they are the foundation of the Book — and others ambiguous."

He said:

> "And none knows its interpretation except Allah and those firmly rooted in knowledge."

In *Hudud al-Ma'arifa* we have set out the position of those who acknowledge *ta'wil* but are ignorant of its proper face; the position of those who reject it and deny it altogether; and the proof against both groups. He also said, concerning the coming of *ta'wil*:

> "Do they await anything but its interpretation? On the Day its interpretation comes, those who had forgotten it before will say: indeed the messengers of our Lord brought the truth."

[^1]: The version (e) reads *Mu'ti* (Giver); the version (S) reads *Mufid* (Bestower / Conferrer), which is the more correct reading.
[^2]: *Da'a'im al-Islam* is one of the books of al-Qadi al-Nu'man, edited in two volumes by Asaf Faydi for the Dar al-Ma'arif press in Cairo, representing Ismaili jurisprudence in its details.
[^3]: In version (S) his description of this is given in fuller form.
[^4]: In version (e) the reading is "the listeners."
[^5]: In version (e) the reading is *la* (negation particle).
[^6]: We found numerous copies of this book in the town of Masyaf, Syria.
[^7]: Al-Sadiq is Imam Ja'far ibn Muhammad ibn 'Ali ibn al-Husayn ibn 'Ali ibn Abi Talib.
[^8]: In version (e) the reading is "and it was advanced."
[^9]: In version (S) the reading is "and his spouse."


<!-- page 28 -->
The Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) said:

"Not a single verse of the Qur'an has been revealed to me without having an outer (1) and an inner (2)."

We mentioned in the book *The Limits of Knowledge* the consensus of the Ummah on the soundness of this report, and the corruption of any interpretation that twists it incorrectly. We also mentioned what those who reject interpretation were nevertheless compelled to acknowledge — unconsciously, because they could not conceive of certain passages without acknowledging an inner sense, and they could find no way out of it. Take, for instance, the word *water*: their own teachings hold that Allah (the Mighty and Sublime) intended by it *knowledge* in one place, and *death* and *disbelief* in another, and *life* in another sense meaning *faith*, and *light* meaning *guidance*, and *darkness* meaning *error*; and that the *blind*, the *deaf*, and the *dumb* mean something other than their outward sense; and that the "sheep of women" — and many others (3) of this sort — are explained at length in this way. Yet when they hear of such things, which are obligatory in order to affirm the absolute oneness of Allah Almighty and to purify Him from any resemblance to His creation, they deny it and liken Him instead. They set up their idol and described Him by what Allah Almighty transcends and is sanctified beyond, and His blessed name is exalted above. They have, after all, heard what the Almighty mentions in His Book about striking parables for His servants. The Almighty says:

"Such are the parables We set forth for mankind, but none understand them except those endowed with knowledge."

And He says:

"Indeed, Allah is not ashamed to set forth the parable of a mosquito or anything above it. As for those who believe, they know it is the truth from their Lord; but as for those who disbelieve, they say,

(1) In copy (E) it appears as "apparent."
(2) In copy (E) it appears as "esoteric."
(3) The word "what" is absent in copy (M).

<!-- page 29 -->
'What did Allah intend by this parable?' By it He misleads many and by it He guides many — and He misleads none except the defiantly disobedient."

And He says:

"And for each We set forth parables, and each We utterly destroyed."

And He says:

"And We have certainly set forth for the people in this Qur'an every kind of parable."

The Almighty also informed us that the prophetic narratives themselves have an interpretation. He said in the story of Yusuf (peace be upon him):

"And thus your Lord will choose you and teach you the interpretation of narratives."

And He said:

"And thus We established Joseph in the land, that We might teach him the interpretation of narratives."

In many verses of His Book He mentions parables, subtleties, and interpretation. This is well known in the tongue of the Arabs, in which the Qur'an was revealed and in which He addressed them. It is among the miracles and wonders of its composition that He brings forth a single utterance which has one meaning in its outward form and another meaning in its inward (1) form. The Almighty made its outward form the miracle of His Messenger, and its inner form the miracle of the Imams of his Household. The inner exists only with them: no one can match the outward of the Book except Muhammad, the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him), who is their grandfather; and no one can match the inner except the Imams from his descendants.

(1) In copy (5), the word "other" appears, which is an addition.

<!-- page 30 -->
It is a knowledge held among them. They speak to each one to the measure he can comprehend, and they give every member of his family what he deserves, and they withhold from one who must be withheld from, and they entrust it to those who deserve to receive it — as al-Wahhab said:

"This is Our gift, so bestow or withhold without reckoning."

Now, with Allah's help and support, we begin to set down what we promised — the interpretation of what we laid out concisely in the book *The Pillars of Islam* — and we present its essence as follows.

This is the boundary at which we begin, in the language of the one whose hearing and measure of understanding you know. Allah (the Mighty and Sublime) said:

"And We did not send any Messenger except in the tongue of his people, so that he might make it clear to them."

For if the general people were addressed in specialized language, they would understand less of the speech than they do. By addressing the audience, we mean to address them at the level of their understanding, and we have not taken them out through the exits used by the people of logic and philosophy, as some preachers have done. Upon Allah we rely; He is our Lord, and He is the One in whose care we are entrusted.

<!-- page 31 -->
**Faith**

We mentioned in the book *The Pillars of Islam* the chapter on faith, and then followed it with a chapter on the difference between faith and Islam. Here we have gathered their interpretations into a single chapter, since at root they share one origin, and this is where we begin.

This is because each of the foremost Messengers (1) (may the prayers of Allah be upon them) instituted two foundations of his law — an outward foundation, and an inward continuation of it — together comprising their boundaries, that is, the limits of the sharia.

So Noah (peace be upon him) instituted the ark, and called those who believed in him to embark with him; whoever did not embark drowned and perished. Ibrahim (peace be upon him) raised up the Ka'bah, and proclaimed the Hajj to the people, calling them to circumambulate it and to perform its rites. Moses (peace be upon him) took up the staff, and cast it at the one who gave him the lie, and it overpowered him from his enchanter. Jesus (peace be upon him) instituted the cross, and said to those who believed in him and trusted him: "Whoever wishes to follow me, let him take up a cross with him." And the foundation of Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the testimony that there is no god but Allah; he summoned the people to it, and they believed in it. He said: "I have been commanded to fight the people until they say with me, 'There is no god but Allah.' If they say it, they have protected from me their blood and their wealth, except for what it rightly requires, and their reckoning is with Allah."

(1) The "foremost in resolve" in Ismaili belief are: Noah, Abraham, Moses, Jesus, and Muhammad. Adam is not counted among them, because the Holy Qur'anic verse was revealed concerning him: "And We had already taken a covenant from Adam before, but he forgot, and We found in him no firm resolve."

<!-- page 32 -->
"There is no god but Allah," and he has entered Paradise.

The outward of these foundations differs — each one is something other than the rest — but their inward is the same. For each foundation contains all the limits of the sharia of its bearer, and all that Allah Almighty sent him with to be worshipped by; otherwise, the one sent would not have summoned people to that foundation, since it would not have been a comprehensive container of all by which Allah is to be worshipped.

The general public of this community claimed that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) called people at the outset of Islam only to bear witness that there is no god but Allah, because that was easier for the people; then, when they answered him, he obliged them to the easier of the obligatory duties, and progressed onward up to jihad — in which souls and wealth are spent — and according to some, the last thing imposed was the Wilayat.

This is a statement contradicted by the wise (1), because it amounts to a form of deception. Where enticement is concerned, one does not invite to a pledge of allegiance, nor establish one's claim to anything, except by laying out the totality of what is required — and the one called to pledge does not pledge allegiance to him unless (2) the sum of what he is obliged to is held at the call. The caller then clarifies it, expounds it, and explains its meanings — as when one calls another to the religion of Islam and that person pledges allegiance to it, since "religion" is a general statement within which obligation is established. Then it becomes clear to the one who answered, and is explained to him. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), when he called for the sincere saying of "There is no god but Allah," made Paradise obligatory for whoever made it known sincerely. He was asked about sincerity in this — what it is — and he said: "Belief in it, and knowledge of its limits."

(1) In both copies the wording reads: "This is a statement that contradicts what the wise man has done." The correct reading is: "A statement that contradicts the wise from doing it."
(2) In copy (E) it appears as "an."
(3) Al-Sadiq is Imam Ja'far ibn Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Husayn ibn Ali ibn Abi Talib, already mentioned.

<!-- page 33 -->
It has its conditions. He said: "The saying 'There is no god but Allah' continues to ward off the wrath of the Lord from this nation, so long as they do not detract from their religion. But when one of them does so, his words will be turned back upon him, and it will be said to him: 'You have lied; you are not among the truthful.'"

Al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) said — among what we have narrated from him in the book *The Pillars of Islam*:

"Faith is a saying with the tongue, a conviction in the heart, and an action with the limbs."

And his saying: "Faith is wholly action, and saying is part of that action," and what he described of the division of faith among the parts of a person — that each of his parts is entrusted with an action that constitutes faith, distinct from what is entrusted to the others — and what he mentioned of all that in a long discourse fully attested in the book *The Pillars of Islam* — all of this makes it necessary that the testimony comprehend the whole of what the sharia requires. It is established about the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) (1) that when he stood against the polytheists he invited them to it; if they answered him and bore witness to it, he brought them under the rule of Islam and ceased fighting them, and their wealth was made safe except for what Islam obliges upon it. If they refused that, he called them to pay the jizya from their own hand while in submission; if they refused that, he fought them. He held that the outward sharia was inseparable from the rule of Islam, by which a person becomes immune in his life and his wealth, except for what is its right. The jizya substitutes for the testimony of "There is no god but Allah" and the acknowledgement of the Prophet; likewise it is necessary for the believer received under a covenant to acknowledge it. This is what Allah (the Mighty and Sublime) said in the story of Ibrahim (peace be upon him): when his Lord said to him, "Submit," he said, "I have submitted to the Lord of the Worlds" (meaning the One who raised him with wisdom). He said: "Death is the Lord of the two distresses." And Ibrahim charged this upon his sons, and so did Yaqub: "O my sons, Allah has chosen for you the religion, so do not die except as Muslims, and I am a Muslim."

(1) Missing in copy (E).

<!-- page 34 -->
Islam is the testimony to Allah by oneness, and to the Messenger by the message. Whoever bears witness to it is obliged to bear witness in truth and sincerity; if he does not, his account is with Allah, as the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: "His testimony has not lost him anything with Allah — if Allah does not save him by it, He still rewards him for it." For it is obligatory in ruling that whoever bears witness to a testimony of which he is not adequate, and does not stand upon its truths, and does not know him for whom or against whom he is testifying — his testimony is not accepted from him, and is rebutted in due course. How then could a testimony be accepted in the greatest of all matters, when it is its postponement? With this great testimony, whose magnitude its bearer testifies to, and whose danger is great, knowing its reality and bearing witness to it outwardly and inwardly, believing in it and fulfilling its obligations and conditions, is obligatory upon the one who testifies to it — only then is it accepted from him, and his reward is not turned away. When he is ignorant of any of it, or denies it, his testimony is not complete, and is not accepted from him.

The one who stands in the secret of the wisdom that Allah Almighty has entrusted to His friends — having made it a miracle for them — sees that the testimony "There is no god but Allah" contained the affirmation of Allah's oneness, the acknowledgement of His Messenger, the Imam of His religion and his limits, the obligation of obedience to them, walaya toward them, and acceptance of their command; it contained all by which Allah Almighty is worshipped. Therefore it became the foundation of the sharia, and the Prophet (peace and blessings of Allah be upon him) called upon it and made Paradise obligatory for whoever testifies to it sincerely. He showed that its sincerity is its belief, the knowledge of its limits, and the performance of its rights — and that the letters of the testimony are what express it, and that what is signified by it is what is within it, since it is an expression of what is uttered. That is why al-Sadiq (peace be upon him) said:

"When Allah first created the letters, He made known by the letters the names,

<!-- page 35 -->
and by the names He made known the people." So empty your hearts of created beings to hear wisdom; accept it, and govern it as it has the right to be governed, and its lights will shine for you, and it will unveil to you its realities and its secrets. For this is the testimony that there is no god but Allah, which is obligatory.

Outwardly, this means that Allah Almighty is the One and only God, with no partner: He is alone in divinity, and He is one as none of His creatures is one. The name "one" may fall upon a creature — and this is so in its outer aspect — but if one returns from it to the inner secondary sense, that creature is paired, and is in need of what it is joined to and compared with: like the human being and the other animals, each of whom is one in number, yet stands as a composite. Likewise, if it is considered as a body and a soul, the body is the outer and the soul is the inner, as we have mentioned above.

In the same way, nothing in the world is without two essences (1), nor without two qualities — either hot and humid, or hot and dry, or cold and humid, or cold and dry; nor without two conditions — either witnessed or absent; nor without two forms — either small or large; nor without two kinds — either animal or human, fine or dense, heavy or light. The contrasts and oppositions are too many to list. The Almighty mentioned all of these clearly from among the conditions of His creation, yet He cannot be described by any of them. He is not non-existent; rather, He exists. He is established as the Sign (2), and the signs are what He created. He is clear of all of them, and nothing resembles Him, and nothing is like Him.

(1) In copy (E) it appears as "for the notables."
(2) In copy (X) it appears as "Pine."


<!-- page 36 -->
Allah Almighty said: "But of it there is one whose interpretation will yet come" — a verse whose meaning will only become clear in its own time.

The general public takes this as a metaphor by which God Almighty addressed the Arabs in their own tongue, since someone who says "he is not like me" or "he is not like so-and-so" who does such-and-such is invoking only the name and nothing further. This is pure analogy, and the Arabs themselves have many proverbs of this kind. And Allah has said: "O son of Adam, obey Me, and I will make you like Myself — living without dying, mighty without being humbled, rich without being impoverished." All of this has an explanation whose remembrance will come, God willing, in its proper place — and were we to elaborate fully here, the exposition of the shahada would be cut short.

## Exposition of the Testimony of Tawhid

The shahada is divided into two clauses: one is negation, the other is affirmation. "There is no god" is negation; "but Allah" is affirmation. Each clause is made of two words: the first joined to the second, and the second elevated above it — and so too the separation between them, which marks the affirmation as higher than the negation that precedes it.

The negation is like the lower limit, and the affirmation is like the upper limit. The first word of the affirmation stands as the highest boundary; the word preceding it stands as the next boundary. The second word of the negation, in turn, stands as the lower boundaries — and similarly the word preceding it, joined to it and yet beneath it.

The two words that join in the limit of affirmation and the limit of negation function as two words and no more; otherwise the joined pair would be loftier and more freighted with meaning, and they would be God Himself.

<!-- page 37 -->
By Allah, this points to the noblest of the upper and lower boundaries, even though each is joined to the others and dependent upon them — and has fewer letters than them — and even though the final word carries three letters that together gather every letter of the shahada: alif, lam, and the disjunctive break. This is the sentence of the saying within the limits of the testimony, which we explain to listeners by the grace of Allah upon us and upon them.

We say: what has been transmitted from the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him), by both the elect and the general public, is that he said: "The first thing Allah Almighty created was the pen. Then He set running a river sweeter than honey, whiter than milk, and softer than butter. Then He created the Preserved Tablet, which Allah Almighty mentioned in His mighty Book."

The general public carried this report toward what they already knew — that pens are made of reed and tablets of wood, as we observe — and did not turn to the fact that these are names, titles, and proverbs that need to be uncovered, unveiled, and explained. This is from the interpretation of the hadith mentioned by Allah, the Almighty.

The pen is the highest limit that Allah has set for His servants — they may not transgress it, as He said: "Whoever transgresses the limits of Allah has wronged his own soul." The Almighty stands above the boundaries and has erected them for His creation beneath Himself, so that they may reach knowledge of Him through them, and that the creation might be led to command and prohibition. The pen is the bearer of knowledge that he derived from Allah (glorified and exalted) and fixed in the Tablet; and the Tablet limited the limits of God beneath itself — meaning that, beneath the pen, the Tablet took its knowledge from the pen, and it became, to it, from it.

These are the two upper boundaries we have mentioned. As for the river that Allah Almighty made to flow for the pen, the Almighty said: "And if they had remained on the right way, We would have given them water in abundance."

The "way" is the prophet who speaks in his time, and the imam in his time after him.

<!-- page 38 -->
The "water" is abundant knowledge. And He said — and He is the most truthful of those who speak: "And We have created above you seven ways, and We are not heedless of creation." We will mention this in its entirety, in its proper place.

This is what the general public has confirmed regarding the interpretation of the esoteric, for they have found no evidence of it. By saying that the way provides water with knowledge, the apparent water is what Allah Almighty causes to descend upon the righteous land and upon the immoral alike — and He has said that if creatures were to stand upright before their guardian, they would have learned the most abundant knowledge.

The saying of the Almighty, "that We may try them therein," means: We test them, see what they say, and teach them about it. Then He said: "And whoever turns away from the remembrance of his Lord, He will lead him into an ever-increasing torment." So He mentioned here that whoever turned away from his guardian, the punishment of God became his — and He called him a "remembrance," that is, a person of remembrance — so the thing was named for him. This is well known in the language of the Arabs: they call a thing by what is suited to it and to its owner. So they call al-maziwada a "narrator" (rawiya), although in their language the narrator is the camel that carries the water — so they named al-maziwada after that camel, because it accompanies him. Such things are many in their language. The Almighty said: "Indeed, Allah has sent down to you a Reminder — a Messenger" — He called the Messenger a "Reminder" because he is the bearer of the remembrance.

Then Allah Almighty established, in correspondence with these two upper spiritual limits, two lower physical limits — namely the *natiq* (the Speaker) and the *samit* (the Silent One). The Speaker (1) Mentioned in copy (S) as "al-Mazwada"; we have found no explanation of this word — perhaps it is Moroccan. (2) The *natiq* is an Ismaili term given to every messenger who came with a message and articulated a law; the speaker-prophets are seven: Adam, Noah, Abraham, Moses, Jesus, Muhammad, and al-Qa'im, the awaited. (3) The *samit* is an Ismaili term given on the basis of the Speaker — he is the Speaker's successor after him, from whom the Imams come in succession; he is the one entrusted with the affairs of the esoteric mission during the Speaker's lifetime, and the foundational *samits* among the Ismailis are likewise seven: Seth, Shem, Isaac, Aaron, Simeon, Ali, and the Mahdi.

<!-- page 39 -->
The Speaker is the master of the sharia, and the Silent One is the foundation of the sharia in the era of the Speaker and the master of its interpretation. For the Messenger utters the manifest, and the foundation is silent about that manifest — that is, from the manifest he conducts toward the subtle, the inner meaning that the Prophet himself has affirmed. This is like the standing of the pen and the knowledge in the Tablet before the pen, and of the Silent One before the Tablet. When the house that God Almighty has established holds these two lower boundaries as a place of transformation, and the bodies are mortal — by the wisdom of God, the house must not be left empty of an argument (hujja). So Allah Almighty established a foundation after the Speaker to take his place in the apparent matter and the other duties the Speaker had been performing, and He instructed him to appoint an argument for himself, to entrust him with the knowledge of interpretation, and to install it with him just as it had been with the Speaker. Then He established an argument after him before him, and ordered him to take an argument for himself in turn — according to the pattern that the Sunnah of Allah Almighty has established, as He said in His Mighty Book: "And you will not find any change in the Sunnah of Allah." This continues until the role of that Speaker turns over seven Imams.

The Speakers, likewise, are seven: Adam, Noah, Abraham, Moses, Jesus, Muhammad, and al-Qa'im of the Age — may God's blessings be upon them. Six of them are designated by a covenant, as He said in His Mighty Book: "And We had already taken a promise from Adam before, but he forgot, and We found in him no firm determination" — indicating that Adam (peace be upon him) has no determination. Every Speaker — whether messenger or imam — has twelve successors in his time, and we will mention the twelve and the seven in due course, God willing.

The four words of the shahada stand as a likeness for these four limits — and whoever recognizes them, accepts them, and bears witness to them completes the limits of the shahada. The shahada gathers them and gathers its boundaries. As for those who affirmed the Creator Almighty

<!-- page 40 -->
by their own claim — from among the philosophers, the logicians, and others — and yet denied the messengers, they affirmed the two upper boundaries and named them by other than these two names: instead of "pen" and "tablet," they called them "the intellect" and "the soul." But they did not know the two lower boundaries, so the shahada was not established for them, because they acknowledged some and denied others.

As for the *zahiriyya* — those from among the "people of refuge" who hold fast to the shahada and the outward marks of Islam, and acknowledge the Tablet, the pen, and the Speaker among the prophets — they did not have proper knowledge of these matters, and they professed something other and so did not complete the shahada. From them this incomplete shahada is accepted only insofar as their blood is spared and their wealth made inviolable; they are also prevented from being among the jizya-payers — as the Prophet established for them by the command of Allah Almighty when they became Muslim.

Islam is a word taken from *salam*, which is reconciliation and surrender — the casting of one's hands into obedience. *Iman* (faith) is a limit that follows Islam: it is knowledge of the limits of the shahada, belief in them, and the rendering of their due — and this is sincerity, as the Messenger of Allah said: "(A.S. Lockham)" — by which he promised the one sincere to it (that is, sincere to the shahada) Paradise, and told us that it is returned to whoever is not sincere to it; one's religion is diminished by however much one's sincerity falls short. So the shahada is the root of the religion — indeed the whole religion is in it — and so the call to it is as we have said.

If we then look at the count of its letters and what it signifies and bears witness to: "there is no god but Allah" comprises seven clauses — and this is a proof and a likeness for the seven Speakers and the seven Imams in the role of every Speaker. These we have already mentioned, and they are the signs of Allah that He mentioned in His Book and in His speech, as He said: "And We made the son of Mary and his mother a sign." Then He said:

<!-- page 41 -->
"Indeed, the Messiah, son of Mary, is the Messenger of Allah and His Word, which He bestowed upon Mary." He also said: "When the angels said, 'O Mary, indeed Allah gives you good tidings of a Word from Him whose name will be the Messiah, Isa son of Maryam.'" He also said: "We will show them Our signs in the horizons and within themselves until it becomes clear to them that it is the truth." And He also said: "And in the earth are signs for the certain in faith, and in your own selves — do you not then see?"

These signs are like those of the shahada, but their likenesses come from heaven and earth. From the side of religion, the heaven is the likeness of the master of the age — whether he is a Speaker or an Imam — and the earth is the likeness of his argument. For water descends from heaven onto the earth, and from it trees and plants sprout, grow, and flourish; so too does knowledge descend from the Speaker to the Silent One, and from it the believers sprout and live.

The likeness of the believers, in these similes of trees and plants, is that they live by the knowledge that comes to them from the argument. And like them too is the sun: in the two heavens (the spiritual and the physical), the sun is like the Speaker who illuminates the world — and the Speaker likewise illuminates the world with his knowledge. Hence the report transmitted from the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him), that he said: "It will rise from its west at the head of the three hundred" — and that was the appearance of the Mahdi.

<!-- page 42 -->
May God bless him with his illuminating light. The moon is like the argument because it draws its light from the sun; likewise the knowledge of the argument is derived from the knowledge of the Imam. The likeness of the Speaker in the body is like the heart, which is the prince of the bodily limbs; and the argument is like the brain, which is the seat of the mind. Each of them is one within the body — and just so, the Speaker is one in the world. Like the Speaker too is the tongue, for through it speech is made; the argument is like the two eyes through which sight is made, like the ears through which hearing is made, and like the two hands through which both restraint and action are accomplished.

From the earth, the likeness is the west and the east. The east is like the Speaker, for from it the light comes; the west is like the argument that receives that light and is where it comes to rest. The seven heavens are like the seven Speakers, and the seven earths are like the seven arguments. Likewise, the seven planetary stars — the *mudabbirat* — that Allah Almighty mentioned in His Book when He said: "And those who arrange the affair" — these are likenesses of the same. So too are the seven climes of the earth, and so too in the human being: the human being was created from seven things, as the Almighty said: "And certainly We created the human being from an extract of clay; then We placed him as a sperm-drop in a firm lodging; then We made the sperm-drop into a clinging clot; then We made the clot into a lump of flesh; then We made out of the lump bones; then We clothed the bones with flesh; then We brought him forth as another creation. So blessed is Allah, the best of creators."

The parts of the human being are seven: hair, skin, flesh, blood, bones, veins, and brain. And his height is seven cubits, and his breadth too — that is, the span between the tips of his two hands when he extends them. He prostrates on seven parts: the face, the hands, the knees,

<!-- page 43 -->
and the feet. In his forehead are seven openings: the eyes, the ears, the nostrils, and the mouth. These are some of the witnesses to the signs of Allah, His limits, and their likenesses and their inward meanings in the greater and smaller worlds. Many further proofs are given at length, and we have mentioned them in *Mention of the Preparations* (*Dhikr al-Muqaddimat*); there is more than this besides, although we have abridged that work as well.

These are the limits that Allah Almighty has commanded be safeguarded, and by which the believers are marked with faith — for they enacted them and preserved them, and by that He made Paradise obligatory for them. He said: "Indeed Allah has purchased from the believers their souls and their wealth, in exchange for Paradise being theirs. They fight in the way of Allah, so they slay and are slain — a true promise upon Him in the Torah, the Gospel, and the Qur'an. And who is more faithful to his covenant than Allah? So rejoice in your transaction which you have contracted, and that is indeed the great success."

Then He described the believers to whom this was enjoined, by their attributes, saying: "Those who repent, who worship, who praise, who fast (or who travel), who bow down, who prostrate themselves, who enjoin the good and forbid the wrong, and who keep the limits of Allah — and give glad tidings to the believers."

These are some of the signs of the seven that we have mentioned. As for the twelve successors — they are *asbab* (instruments / causes) for every Speaker in every age. Four of them are above and closest, and each of them has two — to the right and to the left — making twelve in all. The four of them are like the bird of Abraham in his story, as the Almighty said: "He said: 'Then take four birds and incline them toward yourself.'"


<!-- page 44 -->

They are among the twelve hudud, and they are like the four sacred months (1) according to His saying, exalted is He:

"The number of months with Allah is twelve months in the Book of Allah from the day He created the heavens and the earth, of which four are sacred. So do not wrong yourselves with them."

Their likeness is also found in the testimony, whose letters number twelve. (2) Their likeness is in the twelve constellations of the heavens, and their likeness is in the twelve jaza'ir of the earth — each of them an island — and their likeness is in the twelve spiritual ones, the angels, the messengers, who are the first wings, for it is through them that the command in the message of the Companion is directed. Their wings are the preachers, for each of them is sent to one of the jaza'ir, which has a hadd corresponding to it. For the Almighty has said:

"Praise be to Allah, who made the angels messengers having wings — two, three, and four. He increases in creation what He wills. Indeed, Allah is able to do all things. Whatever mercy Allah opens to mankind, none can withhold; and whatever He withholds, none can release after Him. He is the Mighty, the Wise."

---

(1) The "Harams" according to Ismaili belief are the ministerial consultative council of each Imam, and they number four. One of the four who holds the presidency is called "the Bab," and they are also called "al-Abdal" — that is, "those who have changed" — for they were re-created after their first creation, refined after refinement, as is said in the words of the Ikhwan al-Safa.

(2) These twelve letters together form "la ilaha illa Allah" and correspond to the names of the twelve jaza'ir that make up the globe, or the names of the twelve missionaries called the "missionaries of the jaza'ir," who are charged with the propagation and organization of the da'wa.

<!-- page 45 -->

Understand, then, that the twelve hudud in the lower world correspond to the angels in the upper world. The Almighty has said:

"And of all things We created pairs."

The seven and the twelve together total nineteen, and these are the ones whom Allah has entrusted. The Almighty said: "Over it are nineteen." The testimony has gathered them all together. Whoever knows them, believes in their wilayah, and acts upon what he has taken from them, is a believer among the people of Paradise — accepted in his testimony. Whoever is unable to do any of that, or falls short of it, but bears witness with his tongue, is a Muslim whose testimony is not accepted, and his judgment rests with Allah. For this reason, it is mentioned in the book "Pillars of Islam" from the saying of al-Sadiq (may the blessings of Allah be upon him):

"Faith encompasses Islam, but Islam does not encompass faith. A man may be a Muslim without being a believer, but he is not a believer until he is also a Muslim."

This is the testimony whose hudud we have laid out with respect to the foundations (usul), and connected to it is the second testimony, which pertains to the branches (furu'): "Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him)." It is three words representing the three subordinate spiritual hudud — the upper spiritual ones — namely: Israfil, Mika'il, and Jibra'il.
